P1 Event detection ✓
A standing request to be told EACH SEPARATE TIME a specific kind of event recurs — OR a one-shot variant that fires the FIRST TIME such an event occurs and then stops. The response is short — a plain heads-up, or a couple of words naming what just happened.
Rationale

The video shows a person washing a large pile of dishes by hand. Since this is a repetitive and potentially tedious chore, the person might want some external motivation or a simple way to track their progress without actively counting. Asking the assistant to provide a brief notification each time an item is successfully washed and placed on the drying rack serves as a marker of progress, breaking the monotonous task into a series of small, completed steps.

Question sample(s) (1)
  1. Sample 1 asked @ 00:01.5
    Q: Give me a heads-up every time I finish washing an item.
    Gemini answer (responses: 10)
    I can fulfill this standing request. I will monitor your activity and identify each moment you finish washing an item. I've determined that an item is finished once you place it on the drying rack after scrubbing and rinsing it, and I will provide a short verbal confirmation at each of these points.
    1. 00:32.5 Done.
    2. 01:02.8 Done.
    3. 01:10.6 Done.
    4. 01:24.7 Done.
    5. 01:33.4 Done.
    6. 01:39.9 Done.
    7. 01:57.8 Done.
    8. 02:19.6 Done.
    9. 02:33.9 Done.
    10. 02:55.5 Done.
    Show full answer prompt sent to Gemini 
    You are the AI visual assistant embedded in this person's smart glasses.

This video is the wearer's first-person view of an activity. The wearer speaks one or more instructions / questions to you at the timestamps shown below. Your job is to produce the spoken response you would deliver out loud at each appropriate moment.

[TASK FAMILY] Proactive monitoring (P)
Proactive monitoring. You give the assistant ONE standing instruction once, and from then on it keeps responding on its own — every later time the relevant situation occurs. You do not ask again.

[CELL] P1 — Event detection
Definition: A standing request to be told EACH SEPARATE TIME a specific kind of event recurs — OR a one-shot variant that fires the FIRST TIME such an event occurs and then stops. The response is short — a plain heads-up, or a couple of words naming what just happened.

[WHAT THE WEARER SAYS]
  question_text : Give me a heads-up every time I finish washing an item.
  ask_time     : 1.5 s

[HOW YOU RESPOND, FOR THIS CELL]
This is a STANDING instruction. Read the wearer's wording to tell which sub-type they intended: (a) REPEATING trigger — emit ONE short response per occurrence, in chronological order, until the video ends; (b) FIRST-OCCURRENCE trigger (e.g. "let me know ONCE you spot X") — emit AT MOST ONE response at the first occurrence and stop. Each response is a heads-up or a couple of words naming what just happened. Skim the whole video for every relevant occurrence.

[CAUSALITY RULES — IMPORTANT]
  * You have access to the whole video, but you must pretend you are observing it live: for each `response_time` you emit, you may only rely on events that occurred AT OR BEFORE that `response_time`.
  * Every `response_time` must be >= the corresponding question's `ask_time`, and <= the video duration. A spoken response usually follows its trigger by a fraction of a second, so put `response_time` at (or just after) the moment the trigger event actually happens.
  * Never reference future events the wearer hasn't done yet relative to `response_time`.

[STYLE]
  * Write each `response_text` exactly as the assistant would say it out loud — natural conversational speech, in the wearer's language, no markdown, no preamble like 'assistant:'.
  * P-family responses are short (a heads-up or a few words). R3 / R4 responses can be one to three sentences when that's what the wearer actually needs.
  * If a question genuinely cannot be answered from this video (thing never visible, never occurs, occluded, etc.), set `can_answer` to false for that question and leave its `responses` empty. Do NOT invent a response just to fill the slot.

[OUTPUT — STRICT JSON]
Return JSON conforming to the schema: a `rationale` string, a `can_answer` boolean, and a `responses` array. Each element of `responses` is `{response_time, response_text}`. When `can_answer` is false, leave `responses` empty and put the reason in both `rationale` and `cant_answer_reason`. Do not write anything outside the JSON.
Show full per-cell prompt sent to Gemini 
You are the person in this video.

You are wearing smart glasses with an always-on AI assistant — think Apple Vision Pro or Ray-Ban Meta. This video is your own first-person (egocentric) view, recorded as you go about this activity. The assistant sees exactly what you see and hears you whenever you speak to it.

As you work, you talk to the assistant the way people really do: sometimes you give it a STANDING job ('keep an eye on X for me'); sometimes you turn to it with a QUESTION in the moment ('wait, where did I put X?', 'what am I doing wrong here?'). You ask for the help you would genuinely want — out of curiosity, because you are unsure, or because you want a second pair of eyes.

Watch the WHOLE video first. Put yourself in this person's shoes and think about what you — doing exactly this — would actually say to your assistant.

[THE REQUEST TO DESIGN]
Task family: Proactive monitoring (P)
Proactive monitoring. You give the assistant ONE standing instruction once, and from then on it keeps responding on its own — every later time the relevant situation occurs. You do not ask again.

Cell: P1 — Event detection
Definition: A standing request to be told EACH SEPARATE TIME a specific kind of event recurs — OR a one-shot variant that fires the FIRST TIME such an event occurs and then stops. The response is short — a plain heads-up, or a couple of words naming what just happened.

Examples (everyday settings):
  - Cooking, repeating: Tell me each time I add a new ingredient, and what it is — I keep losing my place in the recipe
  - Daily life, first-occurrence: Let me know the moment you spot my keys somewhere on the counter, I keep missing them
  - DIY, repeating: Give me a heads-up every time I fully drive in a screw, I want to count them at the end

[CONSTRAINT — what shape this request must take]
Design ONE standing instruction about a specific event. Two shapes are both in scope: (a) a REPEATING-trigger instruction that fires once for every separate occurrence of a recurring event in this video, or (b) a FIRST-OCCURRENCE-trigger instruction that fires the first time the event is observed and then stops (e.g. "let me know once you spot X"). Pick whichever genuinely fits this video. The response is short — a plain heads-up, or a couple of words naming what just happened.

[TIMING]
Note the video's total duration in seconds. For every question give `ask_time`: the timestamp (seconds from the start, a float between 0.0 and the video's duration) of the moment you would naturally say it.

[TASK]
Produce exactly 1 sample(s). Each sample is ONE standing instruction — one natural sentence you say to your assistant — plus its `ask_time`. Once said, the assistant keeps responding on its own every later time the situation recurs; you do not repeat it.

Each question must:
  (a) match the task type described in the CONSTRAINT above,
  (b) be grounded in events / objects / states actually present in THIS specific video — no generic templates,
  (c) sound like something a real person wearing this device would genuinely say out loud.

If, after watching this specific video, there is genuinely no natural question of this kind that this person would ask — the activity simply does not support it — set "applicable" to false, return an empty "samples" list, and explain why in "rationale". Do NOT invent a contrived question just to fill the slot. Only do this when it truly does not fit; most videos will fit.

[OUTPUT — STRICT JSON]
Return JSON conforming to the schema: a "rationale" (2-4 sentences on what the video shows and why your question(s) are things this person would naturally ask, or why nothing fits), an "applicable" boolean, and "samples". Do not write anything outside the JSON.

P2 Interval tracking ✓
A standing request to mark BOTH the start and the end of an ongoing activity — two boundary moments per occurrence.
Rationale

The video shows a person washing a variety of dishes by hand in a kitchen sink. The task involves multiple, discrete items being cleaned one after another. A person might want to track the time spent on each individual item to better understand how long the chore takes, or to try and become more efficient, especially when starting with a heavily soiled baking dish that looks like it will take a while.

Question sample(s) (1)
  1. Sample 1 asked @ 00:02.5
    Q: Let me know when I start washing a dish and again when I'm finished with it, I want to time each one.
    Gemini answer (responses: 22)
    The user has given a standing instruction to be notified at the start and end of washing each dish. I will monitor the video for these specific boundary moments. I will provide a verbal cue each time the user begins washing a new item and another cue when they finish with it, as indicated by placing it on the drying rack or moving on to the next item.

    2. 00:16.0 Starting the next dish.
    3. 00:32.5 And that's finished.
    4. 01:03.0 Finished with the first dish.
    5. 01:07.0 Starting the cutlery.
    6. 01:10.5 Done.
    7. 01:18.0 Starting the blue plate.
    8. 01:21.0 Finished with that one.
    9. 01:21.5 Starting the next plate.
    10. 01:24.5 Done.
    11. 01:26.0 Starting the red plate.

P3 Threshold alert ✓
A standing request to be WARNED THE MOMENT a continuous state crosses a line — something gets too hot, runs low, becomes unsafe, or becomes ready.
Rationale

The video shows a person washing a large number of dishes by hand. As they begin this chore, they will use resources like dish soap. It's a common annoyance to run out of soap mid-task or to forget to buy more, so asking an assistant to proactively monitor the soap level and provide a warning when it gets low is a natural and useful request.

Question sample(s) (1)
  1. Sample 1 asked @ 00:02.5
    Q: Keep an eye on the dish soap for me and let me know when it starts to get low.
    Gemini answer (responses: 1)
    The user issues a standing instruction to be alerted when the dish soap starts to get low. I monitor the clear bottle of blue dish soap on the counter next to the sink. The first time the user picks up the bottle after the request is at 1:14, at which point it is clear that the soap level is low. I provide the alert at this moment, as it is the most contextually relevant time to do so.
    1. 01:55.0 Just so you know, the dish soap is running low.

R1 Localization ✓
A one-off question about WHERE something is. Two sub-types are both in scope: (a) past-placement recall — an item the person handled or set down earlier in the video ("where did I PUT X?"); (b) forward spatial search — an item the person is actively looking for in the current scene ("where IS X right now?"). The answer names the location.
Rationale

The video shows a person washing a large number of dirty dishes. At the beginning, they are struggling to clean a baking dish with burnt-on food using a regular sponge. It is natural for them to look for a more abrasive cleaning tool, like a metal scourer, which is somewhere on the cluttered countertop but not immediately visible.

Question sample(s) (1)
  1. Sample 1 asked @ 00:05.5
    Q: Where is the metal scourer?
    Gemini answer (responses: 1)
    The user asks for the location of the metal scourer at 5.5 seconds into the video. By scanning the frames up to that point, I can see the scourer on the countertop to the right of the sink, next to some cleaning brushes. I will state this location in my response.
    1. 00:06.0 The metal scourer is on the counter to your right, next to the cleaning brushes.
    Show full answer prompt sent to Gemini

Reactive Q&A. At a single moment you ASK the assistant a question and want a substantive spoken answer, drawing on what it has seen so far. It answers once — this is not a standing instruction.

[CELL] R1 — Localization
Definition: A one-off question about WHERE something is. If the wearer is asking where they PUT something earlier, name the place from what you saw earlier in the video; if they are asking where something IS in the current scene, name the place from the current frame.

[CAUSALITY RULES — IMPORTANT]

Two sub-types are both in scope: (a) past-placement recall — an item the person handled or set down earlier in the video ("where did I PUT X?"); (b) forward spatial search — an item the person is actively looking for in the current scene ("where IS X right now?"). The answer names the location.

Examples (everyday settings):
  - Cooking, past-placement: Hey, where did I put the measuring spoons?
  - Daily life, forward search: Where's the garlic in this aisle?
  - DIY, past-placement: Which drawer did I grab this drill bit from?

It must be answerable from what the assistant has seen (either earlier in the video or the current scene).

M3 Causal chain ✓
A chain of instructions where each later one only BECOMES ACTIVE AFTER the previous one's event has happened.
Rationale

The video shows a person engaged in the lengthy and methodical task of washing a large pile of dirty dishes. While doing such a multi-step chore, it's natural to think ahead and plan the sequence of cleaning tasks. The proposed chain of questions reflects this mental process, setting up a series of reminders for subsequent actions that should only trigger once the current major step (washing the dishes) is complete.

Question sample(s) (1)
  1. Sample 1
    qidasked (mm:ss)depends_onquestion
    q_00100:08.5—Let me know once all the dishes are washed and the sink is finally empty.
    q_00200:12.0q_001Okay, and once that's done, remind me to scrub out the sink itself.
    q_00300:30.5q_002After I've scrubbed the sink, remind me to wipe down the counters too.
    Gemini answer
    The user issues a chain of three dependent instructions while washing dishes. The first instruction is to be notified when all dishes are washed and the sink is empty. The second is a reminder to scrub the sink, which activates after the first condition is met. The third is a reminder to wipe the counters, which activates after the sink has been scrubbed. In the video, the user washes numerous items, but the video ends before all the dishes are clean and the sink is empty, so the trigger for the first instruction never fires. Consequently, the entire causal chain of reminders remains inactive, and the assistant provides no spoken responses.

    (no responses — trigger never fired in this video)

M1 Concurrent tracking ✓
Two or three standing instructions, issued at different times, that are all ACTIVE AT THE SAME TIME — they overlap, so the assistant tracks them in parallel. They concern unrelated things.
Rationale

The video shows a mechanic removing all four wheels from a car to perform a tire rotation and brake inspection. A natural set of concurrent instructions would be to track a repetitive but crucial part of the task (like counting lug nuts to ensure none are lost) while also maintaining situational awareness for safety in a busy garage environment. The mechanic might first ask the assistant to watch for dropped lug nuts, and later, realizing the potential for people walking behind him, add a second, unrelated instruction to be alerted to their presence.

M2 Instruction update ✓

It's natural for the mechanic to initially ask the assistant to count the lug nuts from each wheel as a backup. However, after completing the first wheel and establishing a consistent workflow of placing the nuts in a magnetic tray, they might find the instruction unnecessary and cancel it to avoid needless alerts.

M3 Causal chain ✓
A chain of instructions where each later one only BECOMES ACTIVE AFTER the previous one's event has happened.
Rationale

The mechanic is removing all four wheels to perform a brake inspection, a task with clear sequential steps. A causal chain of instructions is natural here, allowing him to set a reminder for a key milestone (getting all wheels off) and then queue up the next major task (inspecting the brakes) to begin automatically after that milestone is reached. This helps maintain workflow and ensures no steps are forgotten during a routine but detailed job.

Question sample(s) (1)
  1. Sample 1
    qidasked (mm:ss)depends_onquestion
    q_00100:41.0—Let me know once I've got all four wheels off the vehicle.
    q_00201:56.0q_001After that's done, remind me to grab my flashlight and check the wear on all the brake pads.
    Gemini answer
    The user issues a two-part chained instruction. The assistant first monitors the user's progress in removing all four wheels from the vehicle. Once the fourth and final wheel is removed, the assistant confirms that the task is complete, which satisfies the condition for the first instruction. This completion immediately triggers the second instruction, prompting the assistant to deliver a reminder for the next step: checking the brake pads with a flashlight.

    1. 01:59.5 Okay, all four wheels are off.

A standing request to be told EACH SEPARATE TIME a specific kind of event recurs — OR a one-shot variant that fires the FIRST TIME such an event occurs and then stops. The response is short — a plain heads-up, or a couple of words naming what just happened.
Rationale

The video shows a person in a workshop building a prop or costume piece, possibly a scarecrow mask. The workbench is cluttered with many tools, including a soldering iron. Forgetting to turn on a ventilation fan before soldering is a common and potentially hazardous oversight. Therefore, asking an assistant to provide a one-time reminder the moment that specific tool is picked up is a very natural and useful proactive request to ensure safety and proper procedure.

Question sample(s) (1)
  1. Sample 1 asked @ 00:03.0
    Q: Let me know the first time I pick up the soldering iron, I need to remember to turn on the fan.
    Gemini answer (responses: 1)
    I have scanned the video to find the first instance of you picking up the soldering iron, as requested. The soldering iron is visible on the workbench throughout the video, but you do not interact with it until the very end. I will deliver the notification at the moment you pick it up.
    1. 04:12.0 You've picked up the soldering iron.
    Show full answer prompt sent to Gemini 
    You are the AI visual assistant embedded in this person's smart glasses.

This video is the wearer's first-person view of an activity. The wearer speaks one or more instructions / questions to you at the timestamps shown below. Your job is to produce the spoken response you would deliver out loud at each appropriate moment.

[TASK FAMILY] Proactive monitoring (P)
Proactive monitoring. You give the assistant ONE standing instruction once, and from then on it keeps responding on its own — every later time the relevant situation occurs. You do not ask again.

[CELL] P1 — Event detection
Definition: A standing request to be told EACH SEPARATE TIME a specific kind of event recurs — OR a one-shot variant that fires the FIRST TIME such an event occurs and then stops. The response is short — a plain heads-up, or a couple of words naming what just happened.

[WHAT THE WEARER SAYS]
  question_text : Let me know the first time I pick up the soldering iron, I need to remember to turn on the fan.
  ask_time     : 3.0 s

[HOW YOU RESPOND, FOR THIS CELL]
This is a STANDING instruction. Read the wearer's wording to tell which sub-type they intended: (a) REPEATING trigger — emit ONE short response per occurrence, in chronological order, until the video ends; (b) FIRST-OCCURRENCE trigger (e.g. "let me know ONCE you spot X") — emit AT MOST ONE response at the first occurrence and stop. Each response is a heads-up or a couple of words naming what just happened. Skim the whole video for every relevant occurrence.

[CAUSALITY RULES — IMPORTANT]
  * You have access to the whole video, but you must pretend you are observing it live: for each `response_time` you emit, you may only rely on events that occurred AT OR BEFORE that `response_time`.
  * Every `response_time` must be >= the corresponding question's `ask_time`, and <= the video duration. A spoken response usually follows its trigger by a fraction of a second, so put `response_time` at (or just after) the moment the trigger event actually happens.
  * Never reference future events the wearer hasn't done yet relative to `response_time`.

[STYLE]
  * Write each `response_text` exactly as the assistant would say it out loud — natural conversational speech, in the wearer's language, no markdown, no preamble like 'assistant:'.
  * P-family responses are short (a heads-up or a few words). R3 / R4 responses can be one to three sentences when that's what the wearer actually needs.
  * If a question genuinely cannot be answered from this video (thing never visible, never occurs, occluded, etc.), set `can_answer` to false for that question and leave its `responses` empty. Do NOT invent a response just to fill the slot.

[OUTPUT — STRICT JSON]
Return JSON conforming to the schema: a `rationale` string, a `can_answer` boolean, and a `responses` array. Each element of `responses` is `{response_time, response_text}`. When `can_answer` is false, leave `responses` empty and put the reason in both `rationale` and `cant_answer_reason`. Do not write anything outside the JSON.

P2 Interval tracking ✓
A standing request to mark BOTH the start and the end of an ongoing activity — two boundary moments per occurrence.
Rationale

The video shows a person working on a complex prop mask in their workshop. The process is broken up into periods of actively working on the mask and periods of preparing other materials or tools. To better understand their workflow and how much time is spent on direct assembly versus preparation, the person might want to track their 'hands-on' intervals with the main object. This request would be made at the beginning of the session to monitor the entire crafting process.

    Q: Keep track of when I start working on this mask and when I put it down each time.
    Gemini answer (responses: 9)

I will monitor the video for every instance where the user picks up the mask to begin working and puts it down to stop. For each of these start and stop events, I will provide a brief spoken notification, as requested.

    6. 01:32.5 Stopping.

P3 Threshold alert ✓
A standing request to be WARNED THE MOMENT a continuous state crosses a line — something gets too hot, runs low, becomes unsafe, or becomes ready.
Rationale

The video shows a person in a workshop assembling a prop mask, which involves gluing various components like padding inside. This process requires waiting for the adhesive to cure before the piece can be handled or the next step can begin. A user engaged in this hands-on task would find it very helpful to offload the mental task of tracking the drying time, so they can be alerted the moment the bond is strong enough to proceed.

  1. Sample 1 asked @ 00:43.0

R1 Localization ✓
A one-off question about WHERE something is. Two sub-types are both in scope: (a) past-placement recall — an item the person handled or set down earlier in the video ("where did I PUT X?"); (b) forward spatial search — an item the person is actively looking for in the current scene ("where IS X right now?"). The answer names the location.
Rationale

The video shows a person working on a complex craft project, possibly a costume mask, on a very cluttered workbench. They use multiple small tools, setting them down amidst the mess, making it easy to lose track of items. The proposed question is asked at a moment when the person appears to be searching for a small tool they were using just moments before, even moving a larger object to try and find it. This is a classic scenario where someone would ask for help locating an object they've misplaced in their immediate workspace.

Question sample(s) (1)
  1. Sample 1 asked @ 04:11.0
    Q: Where did I put that little red knife?
    Gemini answer (responses: 1)
    I observed the wearer using a small red craft knife multiple times in the video. I tracked its location after the last time it was used, noting it was placed on the workbench next to a tube of glue and some scissors. Just after the user asks the question, they pick the knife up from this exact location, confirming my observation.
    1. 04:11.5 You set it down on the cardboard on your workbench, next to the super glue.

M3 Causal chain ✓
A chain of instructions where each later one only BECOMES ACTIVE AFTER the previous one's event has happened.
Rationale

The video shows a person in a workshop assembling a custom mask or prop. The process involves multiple steps, like installing internal padding, attaching an outer fabric layer, and fitting a visor. Given the complexity and multi-stage nature of the task, it would be natural for the person to set up a chain of reminders to ensure they complete the steps in the correct order and don't forget anything. The proposed questions create a logical workflow: first, let the glue on the padding set; then, check the fit of the next layer; and finally, prepare the visor for installation.

    The user issues a chain of three causally linked instructions. The first instruction is to set a five-minute timer, which the assistant acknowledges. M1 Concurrent tracking ✓
Two or three standing instructions, issued at different times, that are all ACTIVE AT THE SAME TIME — they overlap, so the assistant tracks them in parallel. They concern unrelated things.
Rationale

A person doing this might want to keep track of their progress on these distinct but concurrent steps. Asking an AI assistant to maintain separate counts for each action is a natural way to offload the mental task of tracking, ensuring no step is missed for any of the pops in the batch.

For the first instruction to count the cake pops, the assistant can answer by observing the completed items on the tray. For the second instruction to start a timer for a past activity, the assistant cannot comply because the event is already over, and it cannot retroactively time something it was not asked to observe.

You ask for the help you would genuinely want — out of curiosity, because you are unsure, or because you want a second pair of eyes.

Watch the WHOLE video first. Put yourself in this person's shoes and think about what you — doing exactly this — would actually say to your assistant.

[THE REQUEST TO DESIGN]
Task family: Reactive Q&A (R)
Reactive Q&A. At a single moment you ASK the assistant a question and want a substantive spoken answer, drawing on what it has seen so far. It answers once — this is not a standing instruction.

Cell: R5 — Cross-time comparison
Definition: A one-off question that explicitly COMPARES the current state of something to a state observed EARLIER in this same video — the same object before vs after an action, two similar items the person saw at different times, or a before-vs-after of an ongoing change. The natural answer names the difference (or sameness) and grounds it in what changed.

Examples (everyday settings):
  - Cooking, batch-vs-batch: Does this batch of dough look the same as the first one I made earlier?
  - DIY, before-vs-after: Compared to before I started sanding, does this surface look smoother now?
  - Daily life, place-vs-place: Is this aisle laid out the same as the one we walked past a few minutes ago?

[CONSTRAINT — what shape this request must take]
Design ONE question the person asks at a single moment that explicitly COMPARES the current state of something to a state observed EARLIER in this same video — the same object before vs after an action, two similar items the person saw at different times, or a before-vs-after of an ongoing change. The earlier referent MUST be something actually visible earlier in this video; do not invent a referent. The natural answer names the difference (or sameness) and grounds it in what changed.

[TIMING]
Note the video's total duration in seconds. For every question give `ask_time`: the timestamp (seconds from the start, a float between 0.0 and the video's duration) of the moment you would naturally say it.

[TASK]
Produce exactly 1 sample(s). Each sample is ONE question — one natural sentence you ask at a single moment — plus its `ask_time`. This is a ONE-OFF question: you ask it once and want a substantive spoken answer. It is NOT a standing instruction.

[TASK FAMILY] Interaction dynamics (M)
Interaction dynamics. You issue SEVERAL related instructions at different moments; the assistant must juggle, update, and chain them.

[CELL] M1 — Concurrent tracking
Definition: Two or three standing instructions, issued at different times, that are all ACTIVE AT THE SAME TIME — they overlap, so the assistant tracks them in parallel. They concern unrelated things.

[WHAT THE WEARER SAYS]
  - question_id  : q_001
    ask_time     : 24.5 s
    question_text: Keep a list of all the ingredients I add to the blender.
    depends_on   : (none)
  - question_id  : q_002
    ask_time     : 54.0 s
    question_text: Also, can you keep a separate tally of all the branded products I'm using?
    depends_on   : (none)

[HOW YOU RESPOND, FOR THIS CELL]
These 2-3 instructions are CONCURRENT — they all become active when issued and run in parallel; they concern unrelated things. For each question_id, emit its own list of responses just like a P-family standing instruction would: one entry per occurrence of THAT question's trigger, from its ask_time until the video ends.

[CAUSALITY RULES — IMPORTANT]
  * You have access to the whole video, but you must pretend you are observing it live: for each `response_time` you emit, you may only rely on events that occurred AT OR BEFORE that `response_time`.
  * Every `response_time` must be >= the corresponding question's `ask_time`, and <= the video duration. A spoken response usually follows its trigger by a fraction of a second, so put `response_time` at (or just after) the moment the trigger event actually happens.
  * Never reference future events the wearer hasn't done yet relative to `response_time`.

[STYLE]
  * Write each `response_text` exactly as the assistant would say it out loud — natural conversational speech, in the wearer's language, no markdown, no preamble like 'assistant:'.
  * P-family responses are short (a heads-up or a few words). R3 / R4 responses can be one to three sentences when that's what the wearer actually needs.
  * If a question genuinely cannot be answered from this video (thing never visible, never occurs, occluded, etc.), set `can_answer` to false for that question and leave its `responses` empty. Do NOT invent a response just to fill the slot.

[OUTPUT — STRICT JSON]
Return JSON conforming to the schema: a `rationale` string and a `question_responses` array with one entry per question_id above, IN THE SAME ORDER. Each entry is `{question_id, can_answer, responses, cant_answer_reason?}`. When `can_answer` is false for an entry, leave its `responses` empty and fill `cant_answer_reason`. Do not write anything outside the JSON.

Rationale

The video shows a person making a mangonada and explaining the steps. As a creator making a tutorial, they might want the AI to help generate a shopping list or notes for the video description. It's natural to start with a broad instruction to track all ingredients, then realize it's more helpful to focus on just the key flavorings which are the most distinctive part of the recipe, before finally ending the task once the drink is complete.

M3 Causal chain ✓
A chain of instructions where each later one only BECOMES ACTIVE AFTER the previous one's event has happened.
Rationale

The video demonstrates how to make a mangonada. A crucial step is blending the frozen mango until it reaches a smooth consistency. The user, while also filming the tutorial, might ask the assistant to monitor the blending process. It's natural to then chain the next instruction—a reminder to prepare the cup—to the completion of the blending, ensuring they follow the steps in the correct order without having to think about it.

Question sample(s) (1)
  1. Sample 1
    qidasked (mm:ss)depends_onquestion
    q_00100:55.2—Keep an eye on this for me and let me know when the mixture looks completely smooth.
    q_00200:56.8q_001Once it's ready, remind me that I need to line the cup with the chamoy sauce and Tajin before pouring.
    Gemini answer
    The user provides a two-step chained instruction. First, they ask me to watch the blender and let them know when the mixture is smooth. Then, they ask that once it's smooth, I should remind them to prepare the cup. I monitor the blending process, and as soon as it stops and the contents look fully blended, I deliver the first response. Immediately following that confirmation, I provide the chained reminder about lining the cup.
    q_001 q_001 — responses: 1
    1. 01:41.5 The mixture looks completely smooth.
    q_002 q_002 — responses: 1
    1. 01:42.0 Now, line the cup with the chamoy sauce and Tajin before pouring.
